B003YFIUW8|2.0|*****************This Review Contains Extensive SPOILERS *****************<br /><br />After seeing, oddly enough, a television commercial for this book my interest was peaked. I went online and perused sites like Amazon and read review after five star review. I mentioned this book to my mother and she literally jumped out of her seat telling me that she'd heard the same and was absolutely DYING to read it. I stopped reading the reviews so as not to spoil myself and immediately picked up a copy. Disappointment is not a strong enough word.<br /><br />The 5 year old child POV narration was little more than a gimmick to distract readers from the thin story. \\"Room\\" is a short novel that could have worked, that *should* have worked. Coming on the heels of a slew of `held in captivity for years' real-life stories over the past 5 years this story had all the elements of a gripping novel. The basic concept is a draw in itself.<br /><br />The real life stories of women such as Jaycee Lee Dugard, Elizabeth Fritzl and Elizabeth Smart and their years-long ordeals are familiar to millions around the world either through interviews or autobiographies. We have been told their harrowing stories of sexual slavery, captivity and depravity, often told in the tone of a 'survivor'. With `Room' we were supposed to be given an account of this kind of ordeal, as it was happening...from the inside. Better still, told from the perspective of the child born out of this degeneracy. I was fully prepared to be riveted.<br /><br />Somehow Donoghue managed to turn an extraordinarily interesting concept into a tedious exercise in endurance. Donoghue had a wealth of information on similar cases like this to work with and yet she did little with it. She outright stole many aspects of various real life `captivity' stories and merely threw them in.<br /><br />Jack's vocabulary and grammar alternate between that of a precocious child who has had years of one-on-one tutoring from a college educated tutor and that of, well, a five year old boy. He jumps from calling jokes \\"Hilarious\\" to saying things like \\"Hotted the air\\" to which his mother only sometimes corrects. It's glaring inconsistencies like these that further add to this books lack of credulity.<br /><br />After being given an account of their monotonous day-to-day lives (in which Jack breastfeeding -- or `having some' as he calls it -- plays a major role) for what seems like `thousands of hours' the story begins to pick up.<br /><br />Following a series of back and forth verbal battles waged between \\"Ma\\" and Jack as to how Jack can escape and get help, they reach a compromise: Jack is to play dead whilst wrapped up in an old rug and thereby enabling him to escape. It seems odd that after a half decade of protecting Jack from whatever danger there was, Ma would simply thrust an unwilling Jack into a most perilous situation.<br /><br />Although it feels a little contrived that `Old Nick' is supposed to simply believe that the boy is dead and carry him off somewhere to bury the body without even checking for a pulse, I suspended my disbelief temporarily as I saw this as a means to an end. Jack escapes in a haphazard, lazily written few pages and encounters a man walking his dog with `Old Nick' fast on his heels.<br /><br />Here Jack only needed to start screaming (an exercise he has been doing almost every day of his life) and asking for help as he's been instructed but he is supposedly so `shell shocked' that he can only stare dumbly at the stranger who holds the key to his very life and safety. `Old Nick' approaches and tries to take Jack back to his truck however the stranger finds this all too suspicious and calls the police. Old Nick flees and after even more sloppy, frustrating writing, \\"Ma\\" is rescued.<br /><br />Part two of the book begins.<br /><br />After suffering though Jack's oftentimes downright annoying inner monologue and dialogue for half the book I felt we were finally going to get somewhere.While their captivity was over, I knew their ordeal was far from it. A story based on this kind of depravity does not simply end if and when the captives are rescued. Questions are to be answered, back-story given etc. Or so I thought.<br /><br />Instead we are treated to some half-assed conversations between \\"Ma\\" and her doctors, the psychiatric hospital staff and Jack himself. None of this answers any questions. Since Jack can't bear to be out of her sight (and vice versa) it's reasonable to assume all of \\"Ma's\\" treatment would take place within his hearing. As such one would also assume we would finally get some insight into exactly what took place before and after her kidnapping or why a nineteen year old college student would be dumb enough to fall for the `lost dog/sick dog' trick. We are not.<br /><br />\\"Ma\\" agrees (voluntarily) to an interview with a television reporter and we also reasonably assume we're going to get some insight there. Instead \\"Ma\\" is abrasive, rude and defensive toward the reporter and rather than give real answers to quite pertinent questions, she uses sarcasm to deflect as if she has been forced into it with a gun to her head. The interview ends and we know no more than we did before.<br /><br />The next day, \\"Ma\\" attempts suicide and is very nearly successful. None of this makes sense, why would a woman who for five years raised her child in an 11x11 room and somehow managed to fiercely protect him from their captor then commit suicide knowing full well that Jack is solely dependent on her for everything? She is rescued just in the knick of time once again, she gets better, the story ends.<br /><br />I must add, while I know I'm in the minority -- even for the low-star reviews -- I have to say it. Jack was annoying. Held in captivity and cut off from society or not he was probably the most annoying little boy in fiction in the past decade. He whined, he cried at every little thing, demanded \\"some\\" from his mother and threw tantrums, inside AND out of \\"Room\\". He was clingy and impertinent and tiresome. I'm not generally a violent person and I know it's not nice to say but I wanted to choke him more than once and wanted to slap him every few pages. I understand everyone's reasons for babying him and I know he grew up in a strange place but god a-mighty I wanted to see him left in a locked room somewhere away from \\"Ma\\" for a good long while, just to have him NOT get his way for once.<br /><br />Donoghue writes the characters of \\"Ma\\" and \\"Old Nick\\" (who could have been arguably two of the most interesting characters in fiction) as one dimensional caricatures of real people who are only there to further serve the story of Jack. Nothing is ever resolved or explained, we never hear from \\"Ma\\" as to how she feels about her captivity or how she got there except for in the most terse, stilted ways, nor do we hear Old Nick's reasons for his behavior.<br /><br />Also, I don't know if Donoghue was championing the breastfeeding of school-aged children or what but when a child is old enough to VERBALLY ask for the breast in complete sentences it is time to stop. Nevertheless I excused this as I assumed it was because Jack had not been immunized at birth. Though \\"Ma's\\" continued breastfeeding after their rescue and \\"Ma\\" not actually giving a reason for it (other than `I had no reason to stop') beggars belief.<br /><br />Ma and Jack share a `special' relationship that caused my eyebrows to knit on more than occasion. She breastfeeds him, they bathe together and they're often naked around each other just because. It feels...wrong, it feels off. Even at the end of the book when the breastfeeding is forced to end, she allows Jack to kiss her bare breasts to `say goodbye'. It made me a little uneasy.<br /><br />What puzzled me the most was that the most interesting character in the book by a LONG shot was \\"Steppa\\", a minor character of little value who actually had a personality. He was interesting; he was funny andseemed to be the most level-headed of all the people in this book. And yet, we see so little of him. From his character it's clear that Donoghue KNOWS how to write a proper character and give back-story, she just chooses not to.<br /><br />I'm sorry to say this book is simply not very good and I can't find a single reason to praise it.I know it's not nice to say but I wanted to choke him more than once and wanted to slap him every few pages. I understand everyone's reasons for babying him and I know he grew up in a strange place but god a-mighty I wanted to see him left in a locked room somewhere away from \\"Ma\\" for a good long while, just to have him NOT get his way for once.     Donoghue writes the characters of \\"Ma\\" and \\"Old Nick\\" (who could have been arguably two of the most interesting characters in fiction) as one dimensional caricatures of real people who are only there to further serve the story of Jack. Nothing is ever resolved or explained, we never hear from \\"Ma\\" as to how she feels about her captivity or how she got there except for in the most terse, stilted ways, nor do we hear Old Nick's reasons for his behavior.     Also, I don't know if Donoghue was championing the breastfeeding of school-aged children or what but when a child is old enough to VERBALLY ask for the breast in complete sentences it is time to stop. Nevertheless I excused this as I assumed it was because Jack had not been immunized at birth. Though \\"Ma's\\" continued breastfeeding after their rescue and \\"Ma\\" not actually giving a reason for it (other than `I had no reason to stop') beggars belief.     Ma and Jack share a `special' relationship that caused my eyebrows to knit on more than occasion. She breastfeeds him, they bathe together and they're often naked around each other just because. It feels...wrong, it feels off. Even at the end of the book when the breastfeeding is forced to end, she allows Jack to kiss her bare breasts to `say goodbye'. It made me a little uneasy.     What puzzled me the most was that the most interesting character in the book by a LONG shot was \\"Steppa\\", a minor character of little value who actually had a personality. He was interesting; he was funny and seemed to be the most level-headed of all the people in this book. And yet, we see so little of him. From his character it's clear thatDonoghue KNOWS how to write a proper character and give back-story, she just chooses not to.     I'm sorry to say this book is simply not very good and I can't find a single reason to praise it.

B003UYV08E|1.0|Iron Man 2 had all the elements of a blockbuster movie. A strong cast, solid direction by Jon Faverau, and spectacular special effects. Unfortunately, the film is bogged down by a soulless poorly written script that's all over the place. One sequel into this franchise and Iron Man is looking rusty.<br /><br />Iron Man 2 picks up where Iron man 1 left off. Six months after telling the world He's Iron Man, Tony Stark is now dying of the palladium in the arc reactor that is keeping him alive. On top of this, the U.S. government is trying to take the technology for the Iron man armor from him, and competitors like Justin Hammer are trying to replicate the armor.<br /><br />But somewhere in Siberia...the son of a former Stark employee Anton Vanko, Ivan Vanko has the secret arc reactor recipe that powers the suit. As Stark confidently testifies to the congresss that he's the only person who has the technology, Vanko heads over to France with his powered whips and own version of the Stark's secret recipe for the arc reactor.<br /><br />After the big display at the Stark Expo, a dying Stark keeps his secret as he heads to France decides to race his own car. This is where the wheels fall off the movie and it becomes a jumbled mess. It's clear this sequence is just conveniently there so Whiplash can wreck stuff and Iron man can beat him up. I rolled my eyes as Happy Hogan drives down the race track the wrong way to give Tony his red and silver Mark V suit to beat up Whiplash and deliver him to the French police.<br />After Tony returns from France, we get more tacked on plot points in his new secretary Nancy Rushman who beats him down in his boxing ring. As he banters with Pepper Potts more convoluted stories are started. Nick Fury shows up with a mystery regarding some element Howard Stark was working on. Ivan Vanko gets busted out of Jail by Justin Hammer. Hammer goes to get his \\"burd\\" as the plot meanders to the midpoint.<br /><br />A few sequences later it's Tony's birthday, and a drunken Stark celebrates by putting on his Mark IV armor and blasting stuff. Realizing he's putting people in danger, Jim Rhodes (again coming out of nowhere) suits up in the Mark 2 suit to stop him. After a big rock-em-sock-em robot fistfight between the armored gladiators, and an repulsor blast explosion that shatters all the glass in Tony's house, Rhodes announces he's taking the suit and flies away. As this tacked on plot point moves the movie forward, Shawn tries to figure out how he learned how to fly an Iron man suit, accessed the pass codes between movies. He also tries to figure out how a suit with no repulsors has them.<br /><br />As Rhodey delivers the Mark 2 suit to the government to be weaponized, Tony goes to get some donuts, Nick Fury drops by to put Tony under House arrest. In addition to giving him something to do while cleaning up all that broken glass, he's told to figure out the mystery of the element. Tony goes and watches home movies, breaks out a model of the old Stark Expo and begins to solve the mystery of the triangle.<br />Meanwhile Vanko and Hammer are putting together drones. But Vanko has an eleventh herb and spice he's not telling Hammer about.<br /><br />Meanwhile at the Stark mansion, Tony wrecks his house finding out the secret of the Triangle. As he installs the new coconut flavored element into his arc reactor, his life is saved just in time to figure out what Ivan Vanko is up to with his eleventh herb and spice.<br /><br />At the Hammer expo, Hammer does the Hammer dance as he reveals the drones and Jim Rhodes as War Machine. Vanko reveals the last part of his secret recipe. As Iron man enters in his shiny new Mark VI suit, he confronts Hammer and the drones start attacking.<br />From here, Nancy Rushman goes into action as the Black Widow, and takes out Hammer's guards to get to Vanko.<br /><br />But Vanko is long gone and she has to hack the computer codes get control of the drones. All she does is get War Machine back under Rhodey's control. Well, he needs to help tony beat up some robots. After destroying all the drones, Here comes Vanko in his souped up Crimson Dynamo knock off armor. After a heavy metal beatdown, Tony and Rhodey fire repulsors at each other, Vanko is KO'd. Vanko knowing he's beaten decides to make himself and the drones blow up just in time for tony to save Pepper.<br />At the end of the movie, Tony gets a medal for beating up on the Hammer drones, credits roll and we see a hammer and Agent Colson telling Nick Fury he's found it. I'd like to find out what happened to the story in this movie. Somewhere around the fifteen minute mark it stopped making sense.<br /><br />It's clear there's something missing in Iron Man 2; it doesn't have the heart or spirit of the first film, which had a light lively tone. By contrast this film feels artificial and manufactured. It's clear the studio executives and the toy companies meddled in the creative process for this film to sell as many T-shirts, action figures and whatever merchandise they could imagine. Instead of the plot flowing together organically like the first film, Iron man 2 is jam-packed with characters that serve no purpose and a jumbled plot that goes nowhere. Tony's armor gets an upgrade but the franchise needs a downgrade. This movie is too much like the jumbled storyline of a comic book and nothing like the linear pacing of a movie.<br /><br />As a diehard Iron man fan I was deeply disappointed with this film. As a Movie fan I was disgusted. Usually the process of executive meddling that ruins a franchise starts with the third movie.<br /><br />I can't fault the actors here, they were prevented from doing their best with the horrible script they were given. Robert Downey Jr. is solid here but is held down from greatness due to the bad script. Gwyneth Paltrow who was amazing as Pepper Potts last time is awkward here. She and Downey don't have the electric Cary Grant/ style chemistry they shared in the first movie. the Don Cheadle is wooden as Jim Rhodes. Terrence Howard had much better energy and charisma in the role. Cheadle has no chemistry with Robert Downey Jr, and is just flat in the role. Sam Rockwell is solid as Justin Hammer. I'm still trying to figure out what Scarlett Johansson's purpose was in playing the Black Widow. Her part was severely underwritten and underdeveloped. Samuel L. Jackson is passable as Nick Fury. But again, they give him nothing really serious to work with.<br /><br />Director Jon Faverau does his best to maintain the integrity of the Iron Man Franchise. The shots are about the same, the sequences are consistent with the first film, but it's clear the film doesn't have the same energy. What was once a fun superhero action franchise looks like it's on the slow road downhill like Sam Raimi's Spider-Man franchise after Spider-Man 2 and Spider-Man 3. All I know is you won't see me in line for Iron Man 3. If you're thinking about getting Iron Man 2 Pass on it. This franchise is getting rusty.bots. It celebrates the boredom of over-choice and over-indulgence - no it doesn't \\"celebrate\\" anything - it cynically wallows in it. It is wearing to read - it no more builds a narrative muscle than television does. After forcing myself through two of the books (I prefer not to write a bad review of something without making sure I have a good familiarity with what I am criticizing) I felt the same way I feel when I grossly over-sleep - a feeling of past gluttony and disgust. There is so much great literature out there for children. Reading for the sake of reading is pointless. It matters WHAT you read. The argument that this gets children reading when they are not so inclined is wrong-headed. Holding a conversation with a friend - even a friendly dog - would be more fulfilling than this kind of cynicism and self-indulgent rhetoric. It stops being funny after the first 10 pages - just like TV sitcoms that can no longer be aired without a laugh track. Give your children GOOD books to read. There are so many to choose from. It matters. Don't let them waste their time with this - they would be better off messing around in the woods or lounging around with friends. The fact that this book is popular is a pathetic reflection on our culture. Quality matters - it matters in all things.

Her inner goddess is the funniest part of this novel. I love her.    This novel also has some SURPRISING TWISTS & TURNS. E.L. James keeps you engaged and waiting to see what will happen next. James is an extremely talented author. She knows how to write a sexy, suspenseful, amazing novel.    I gave Fifty Shades Darker by E.L. James 5 STARS. I strongly recommend the Fifty Shades trilogy (third book not yet released) to every adult who can handle a dark, graphic, intense, lovely, witty, sexy romance.

B0037TRVD0|5.0|My exposure to the truly Incredible String Band began with this singular album. While I have since grown to love \\"Wee Tam/Big Huge\\", \\"5000 Spirits\\" and Mike Heron's \\"Smiling Men with Bad Reputations\\", this remains their most accomplished and bewitching musical statement in my opinion.     I've heard it said that this is very much a product of the 1960's, and perhaps somewhat dated today. I maintain that while the attitudes of the 1960's allowed for such a record to be made, \\"The Hangman's Beautiful Daughter\\" is a truly timeless album. It is a very human album, rooted in sensations, memories, mythology, ideas and emotions that imbue it with a sense of familiar continuity. That is, once the listener can absorb the global folk music approaches and sounds that have been amalgamated into the ISB sound. These can range from the Italian sounding harpsichord Oom Pa Pa on \\"Waltz of the New Moon\\", to the Arabic scales used for the vocals throughout the second half of the album. From the Bahamian spiritual incorporated beautifully into \\"A Very Cellular Song\\", to the sitar and gimbri that add colour and dimension to many of the melodies. While the combination of so many influences can make this album a bit daunting to approach, they ultimately ensure that it is not solely of one time or place.     To be sure, this is haunted music if ever there was such a thing, but it is also by turns whimsical, wistful and reverential. It addresses some of the most basic (though not simple) human thoughts and desires. And by God, when Heron sings \\"turn your quivering nerves in my direction!\\", my nerves most definitely do quiver in response. Which just goes to show you, if you are willing to open your ears and hearts, this is music that can move you at the most basic (though not simple) level.

Stereo Splitter (3.5mm) Audio Adapter   $2.99   Qty: 2   Sold by: BlueMall  Payment Method:    Items: $5.98   Shipping & Handling: $5.96    which is a male RCA adapter (those red and white plugs used to input output audio) with a female 3.5mm headphone jack. Instead adding some half-baked excuse of a graphic \\"extra\\" character walking in the background is immensely distracted in low resolution situations; it is tenfold worse exploded on a larger high definition screen. He needs to simply release the originals, in HD, period. Nothing else, nothing extra is needed. I don't want special features, or deleted scenes, I don't want anything of that nature, and that's what the fans--at least I believe as much--feel. All this time and money has been expended for something denaturalized from its original form, it isn't an improvement of the old, but some sick twisted mutation, a foggy image of what once was. Perhaps that's saying a lot about George Lucas at this point in his career, maybe he should just get out of the business all together.

B00005JLKN|4.0|After the disappointment that was `Hannibal', I really wasn't expecting anything substantial out of `Red Dragon'.  I have not seen the original adaptation, `Manhunter', so I had nothing to base my expectations on outside of the incredible `The Silence of the Lambs' and the horrendous free-fall `Hannibal'.  What I got was something far grander than I expected.  It is laced with a familiar feeling that can allude to something along the lines of `Law & Order: Criminal Intent' at times, but overall this is a tout thriller that delivers the chills.<br /><br />Much like in `The Silence of the Lambs', Hannibal Lector himself is not the chief `villain' here, but merely a sounding board and advice column for an FBI agent, this time a male by the name of Will Graham.  The villain this go around is The Tooth Fairy, a disturbed serial killer covered in a dragon tattoo.  Graham, battling his own demons (aren't we all) uses Hannibal to track down this savage killer.  Hannibal and Graham have their own history though, and it is that history that plays an integral role in the advice Graham receives.<br /><br />Ralph Fiennes is extraordinary here.  He is truly terrifying; chilling to the bone.  This was released the same year that Fiennes plays another disturbed soul in Cronenberg's `Spider'.  I feel that his portrayal of The Tooth Fairy is far more complex and effective.  Edward Norton is one of my favorite actors, but he doesn't pop as much here; especially when he's sandwiched between Fiennes and Hopkins, both of whom steal spotlight rather effortlessly.  I'm happy to see Hopkins more effective here than in `Hannibal', though I'd be lying if I said I was happy to see him continually reprise this role.  I don't know, I mean; I'm one who says that if you play a role once and a sequel or whatever is made then you should do it again, but there is something about this franchise that feels a tad unnecessary.  Maybe it's because Demme's 1991 masterpiece was so unexpectedly and universally lauded that the subsequent sequels (and prequels) feel like cheap attempts at capitalizing off the initial success, especially with Hopkins.  Maybe I should blame the rest of his career, which at times feels like it's always trying to remind us of his turn as Hannibal (countless movie posters using his face and that `look' as a way to pull in the crowds).  I feel like I've seen him do this enough and I don't want to see him do it again.  An actor as brilliant as Anthony Hopkins should never be reduced to a variation of a singular character.<br /><br />That's enough ranting.<br /><br />Outside of a few glitches and an ending that was clearly designed to illicit a desired reaction that is a bit of a cop-out as others have called it, `Red Dragon' is a well-rounded thriller that isn't an embarrassment to its source material.  Fiennes is dynamic, and even supporting performances from Emily Watson and Philip Seymour Hoffman make an impact.<br /><br />It's better than you think it'll be.the subsequent sequels (and prequels) feel like cheap attempts at capitalizing off the initial success, especially with Hopkins. B004SB60OC|5.0|Christina Perri is such a refreshing talent.  She comes across humble, content with delivering great music and not preoccupied with catching our eye with the unessentials.  She focuses her energy on writing, and what we are left with is a collection of supremely inspired songs that are layered with emotional depth that is backed up with intelligent and inspired lyrics.<br /><br />I'm so excited to review this album!<br /><br />`Bluebird' opens the album flawlessly.  The songs theme, which tells of a love triangle that is taking its toll on Perri emotionally, is impeccably tailored with such allegorical mastery.  I loved the likening of the `other woman' to a bluebird.  Perri also throws blame in the right direction (\\"if he was something special I wouldn't have this song\\") while accepting her inability to do the right thing by herself (\\"what if when she comes over I am in your arms taking all I want from you again\\").  `Arms' seriously gave me chills.  As Christina sings softly about the love of her life, the one who she didn't expect but who swooped in and saved her, you can't help but feel such emotional connection.  The lyrical content here is so sublimely crafted, with astute words that help create a well-rounded understanding of her `love', right down to the insecurities that corrode her happiness (\\"I believe that it's easier for you to let me go\\").<br /><br />`Bang Bang Bang' has such energy, with those bouncy piano keys and that explosive chorus.  I love the way she takes the subject (relishing the karma that strikes and ex beau) and ignites it with a chant worthy chorus (\\"bang she shot you, karma tastes so sweet\\") while never negating her knack for piercing verse structure (\\"you lied and you lied and I died and I dies and now you know why\\").<br /><br /> `Distance' plays out beautifully, as Christina sings about that king of love that you have to keep secret for fear of losing it.  As the male vocals slip in to elevate the chorus line (\\"say I love you while you're not listening\\") the song just swells with such restrained intensity.  It is a heart touching song about loving the wrong person, or possibly the right person who is just willing to accept that he (or she) loves you too.  `Jar of Hearts' is the first song the world heard from Perri.  It is impressively haunting, even now that it's been played and played and played out.  The imagery that comes from her words (\\"who do you think you are, running round leaving scars, collecting your jar of hearts and tearing love apart\\") is truly inspired and leaves lasting impression, and the way she delivers it with such solemn intensity over those haunted piano keys is just devastating.  `Mine' shows a more edgy and upbeat side to Perri.  She even inserts a slightly sinister atmosphere into the way she repeats that word; \\"mine\\".  It's fun and bouncy, and the musical breakdown (I love the fluctuations) is completely engaging.  That total feeling of losing the control you thought you had (\\"how did this happen and why are you laughing and how do I get back together again?\\") really illuminates the close of the song extremely well.<br /><br />`Penguin' is so soft and sweet and gentle moving; and I just have this utter adoration of penguins, and so likening true love to those cuddly little creatures was such a marvelous metaphor (\\"like a soul mate, he's your penguin\\").  The track is very simple and really rests on Perri's soft delivery (she almost whispers the song).  The song is ethereal, especially the closing bridge where Perri's voice pierces with the enchanting chanting of \\"let go\\".<br /><br />The song feels special.<br /><br />`Miles' is extremely catchy, but in that vein it is also somewhat familiar.  It doesn't stand out as much as the other tracks here.  Instead, it sounds like a B-Side off of a Sara Bareilles album or something like that.  Even the chorus line (\\"don't count the miles, count the `I Love You's'\\") feels a tad stale and uninspired, and especially disappointing when you consider the staggering depth she brought to nearly every other song here.  `The Lonely' sends shivers down my soul.  She just bleeds with such emotional devastation here; certainly her most haunting track, even more debilitating than `Jar of Hearts'.  The rise given to the chorus (that piano work) really gives the track an almost orchestral quality.  She really captures the realities of loneliness (\\"I'm the ghost of a girl that I want to be most\\") with such curious intensity; and that singular line about \\"dancing slowly in an empty room\\" is visually shattering.  `Sad Song' is my least favorite track here because it seems to be trying too hard (\\"today I'm gonna write a sad song...so that everyone can see that I'm very unhappy\\").  It's just too straightforward for its own good.  Telling us that this is a sad song doesn't necessarily make it sad, and the bouncy musical shift for the chorus is an odd match to the solemn verse structure.  I understand the lyrical content, but I found it a little too conventional; but I'll admit that Perri's vocal's make the song enjoyable regardless of the `immature' feeling I get from it.<br /><br />`Tragedy' closes out the album allows Christina to show such vulnerability in her voice, in the process embellishing her vocal range.  I love the way she starts her words without completely finishing them, allowing the break of her voice to finish the thought.  The song gets into the cracks of a relationship, and the sharp pains that come from realizing for yourself that this was the wrong thing to get wrapped up in (\\"why did you feel the need to prove that everybody else was right\\").<br /><br />It's a stunning way to close out the album.<br /><br />I highly recommend this album, for it is certainly one of the best of the year!<br /><br />For those of you interested; my personal track ranking:<br /><br />1) Distance<br />2) Arms<br />3) Mine<br />4) Penguin<br />5) Bluebird<br />6) Tragedy<br />7) Jar of Hearts<br />8) Bang Bang Bang<br />9) The Lonely<br />10) Miles<br />11) Sad Songrly every other song here.   `The Lonely' sends shivers down my soul.  She just bleeds with such emotional devastation here; certainly her most haunting track, even more debilitating than `Jar of Hearts'.  The rise given to the chorus (that piano work) really gives the track an almost orchestral quality.  She really captures the realities of loneliness (\\"I'm the ghost of a girl that I want to be most\\") with such curious intensity; and that singular line about \\"dancing slowly in an empty room\\" is visually shattering.  `Sad Song' is my least favorite track here because it seems to be trying too hard (\\"today I'm gonna write a sad song...so that everyone can see that I'm very unhappy\\").  It's just too straightforward for its own good.  Telling us that this is a sad song doesn't necessarily make it sad, and the bouncy musical shift for the chorus is an odd match to the solemn verse structure.  I understand the lyrical content, but I found it a little too conventional; but I'll admit that Perri's vocal's make the song enjoyable regardless of the `immature' feeling I get from it.    `Tragedy' closes out the album allows Christina to show such vulnerability in her voice, in the process embellishing her vocal range.  I love the way she starts her words without completely finishing them, allowing the break of her voice to finish the thought.  The song gets into the cracks of a relationship, and the sharp pains that come from realizing for yourself that this was the wrong thing to get wrapped up in (\\"why did you feel the need to prove that everybody else was right\\").      It's a stunning way to close out the album.    I highly recommend this album, for it is certainly one of the best of the year! 0439023521|3.0|A couple of persuasive people convinced me to read this book. It was a pretty decent read, although I was initially bothered by its unoriginality; It's essentially the protagonist of Winter's Bone (A somber, no-nonsense teenage girl living in the economically repressed Appalachians with an absent/dead father and a catatonic mother who is forced to hunt squirrels and other animals to provide for her younger siblings) inserted into the plot of the Japanese movie Battle Royale (Teenagers drafted into a reality television show set in the woods and forced to fight each other to the death). But the importance of originality can be overstated-the main point of the story is not WHAT it is about, but HOW it is about it (I think I stole that from Roger Ebert).    And I don't think either of those other works holds much appeal for a lot of young women anyway.    Hunger Games is a definite page turner, but unfortunately it isn't a wholly satisfying read-even by `young adult' standards. I got the impression that the author is more motivated by book sales and movie deals than providing a complete story experience for her reader. The book ends abruptly and you have to buy the rest of the trilogy to find out what happens. Perhaps this is the main reason for the lack of any serious characterization with the story's protagonist, Katniss. She's not terribly deep or insightful; at times her response to the whole battle to the death thing seems to border on nonchalance.    This is likely because Hunger Games: The Motion Picture was always meant to be marketed as an action-romance movie (rated PG-13!) and too much insight would slow things down. Plot is all you really need to make money. But Katniss is likable enough to carry the story and Collins does a capable job of highlighting her character's inner strength and craftiness and making her endearing.    Up until the arbitrary ending, I enjoyed it. If I had a young daughter (I would guess the targeted demographic here is between 10 and19), I would give her a copy of the book with the hope that she could find some inspiration in a tough female character who climbs trees, hunts rabbits and has more important things to do than obsess over getting a boyfriend.

1439156816|5.0|Stephen King knows how to write. He's been around a while and he's sold lots and lots of books, which I knew, but I'd never read any of them myself. The type of literary horror he's known for was never my thing. But after a friend convinced me to read King's memoir `On Writing,' I can understand why the guy is so popular.    The first half of `On Writing' is an autobiography. Again, not really my thing, but King drew me in. Most of the anecdotes about his \\"odd, herky-jerky childhood\\" range from funny to hilarious, and true to the title of the book, he involves the reader in the process of writing an autobiography as he does it, describing what it feels like trying to sort through his lifetime of unorganized memories.    The second half is where he actually writes about writing. I've read very few books about writing since composing sentences and stories always seemed to be something you just had to try; I never understood what knowledge a book about writing could impart that couldn't be discovered through my own attempts.  I was also weary of the kind of spiritual ridiculousness that some people attribute to creative pursuits, but King seemed to anticipate this concern, stating in his preface: \\"This is a short book because most books about writing are filled with bulls--t.\\" He won me over right away.    King keeps his writing advice succinct and tangible. My favorite two rules: Don't use a thesaurus because the first word you thought of is the best, and avoid adjectives because as a writer you should be showing instead of telling.    (Example: `\\"Stop that,\\" John said angrily.' That's a stupid sentence because a reader should be able to hear John's anger without being told it's there.)    Though King isn't unrealistically encouraging--he states a belief more than once that a bad writer cannot be made into a great one--he does a great job of sharing his love and enthusiasm for his craft. He made me want to write more and I plan to keep a copy of his book around for reference.  I'll also always keep in mind another piece of his advice:    \\"If you want to be a writer, you must do two things above all others: read a lot and write a lot.\\"

That movie was awful--so yes, I guess this movie does have something in common with that one.       Where do I start?  The movie 'Something Borrowed' was flat and disjointed. The actors themselves did an okay job, but considering who was on board I expected so much more. Kate Hudson fared better--though her appearance is a complete contrast to Darcy in the book, she was the most successful in bringing life to the character. Colin Egglesfield as Dex was just there; I don't particularly think he added or detracted anything, which is not really good since he's supposed to be a huge part of this story.  I don't know if he could have done more based on what he was given to work with because the script cut out a lot of the development of Dex & Rachel's relationship.  And I guess that is true for all the actors--they should have been great instead they were just \\"eh\\".      One ofthe few bright spots in this movie is John Krasinski.  His character was elevated from the minor role in the book in order to provide much needed comic relief but also to set up for the sequel that's in the works (which should hopefully be a little more successful if for no other reason than John Krasinski will be the lead). B000PMLFRA|5.0|\\"This will be a day long remembered.\\" This quote from the first Star Wars film, known since 1981 as \\"Episode â£: A New Hope,\\" describes the feelings of myself, and countless other Star Wars fans on the eagerly awaited Blu-ray release of the Star Wars saga. I just received mine about two hours ago as of this writing. But with every new release of Star Wars comes a looming question: What will George Lucas change this time?<br /><br />This was not always the case. Fifteen years of home video releases saw the trilogy presented exactly as they were seen in theaters (albeit usually in panned and scanned versions formatted to fit a standard television screen), and with the release of 1993's THX edition release (available in widescreen for the first time on VHS), it seemed the movies would never be better than they were.<br /><br />The work Industrial Light & Magic did for Steven Spielberg's Jurassic Park changed all that. Lucas admitted that he was never happy with the way Star Wars came out, due mostly to restraints of time, money, and ILM's pioneering, yet crude technology, and announced that with current computer graphics, he could finally go back and complete scenes from the original trilogy that had bugged him for years.<br /><br />1997 saw the release of the Star Wars Trilogy Special Edition, a theatrical (and eventually home video) re-release of all three films with newly enhanced special effects, and a handful of additional scenes. To say the reaction was mixed would be an understatement. Nearly a decade-and-a-half after they were revealed to the world, many of the changes Lucas made have been heavily criticized and hotly debated. Those who grew up on the originals felt that the new additions ruined the movies. Disappointed fans took solace in the fact that at least they could still watch the original versions of the films at home on VHS.<br /><br />Seven years later saw the DVD release of the \\"Original Trilogy\\" as it was now known, following two prequels, with one last film on the way. Fans pleaded and prayed that Lucas would see fit to release the theatrical versions alongside the Special Editions, which were now said to have even more changes to better tie in the Originals with the Prequels. Not only did they not get their wish (at least not until a few years later), but many fans scoffed at some of the newest changes made to the series, believing the prequels to be further ruining the series, and any changes made to accommodate those films would be even more detrimental to the dignity of the Original Trilogy.<br /><br />So here we are today. I have my Star Wars Blu-rays laying on my sofa, awaiting their weekend marathon. I have heard rumors of even more changes to Episodes â£ through â¥, as well as a few for â  through â¢. These rumors include deleted scenes incorporated into the films, a new CG Yoda to replace the puppet (I knew they were doing this for Episode â , though I'm still unsure about whether this plan also includes Episodes â¤ and â¥), and the possibility of Han once again shooting first. I will thank anyone who has knowledge of exactly what changes have been made to keep quiet. I want to be surprised.<br /><br />The thing is, after all these years of changes and complaints, I still don't see what the big deal is. There are very few changes to the Original Trilogy to which I strongly object, and none of those changes affect the over-all film in any way. What difference does it make whether Han or Greedo shoots first? As long as Greedo misses, the other approximately 13 hours, 20 minutes, and 59Â¾ seconds of the saga remains roughly the same. Sure, the Jabba the Hutt scene in A New Hope seems redundant and unnecessary in terms of the single film, and maybe it ruins the \\"big reveal\\" of Jabba in Return of the Jedi, but since Jabba already appears in The Phantom Menace (canonically the first film in the saga), and that scene was always supposed to be in A New Hope in the first place, it hardly ruins anything. I will agree, Jedi Rocks sucks, Lapti Nek is better, but since it is only a nonsensical musical number with no relation to the plot, whichever song they use is more personal preference than pertinence. Speaking of music, I also prefer the instrumental music and galactic celebrations used at the end of Jedi now to the \\"Yub Nub\\" teddy bear picnic from the original. And as for replacing Sebastian Shaw with Hayden Christensen... it could have been done better, but again, it's only a few seconds of footage, and for the new generation of Star Wars fans who have grown up with the prequels, it does help tie the whole story together.<br /><br />I have yet to even look at the Blu-rays (as of this writing), and I will not see anything from Episodes â£ through â¥ until Sunday, but when I do I will reserve judgement, and determine for myself if anything \\"ruins\\" Star Wars. I doubt anything will. I might not agree with some of Lucas' decisions, but when it comes right down to it, they're not my -- nor anyone else's -- movies, but his. I challenge anyone who says that they could make a better movie than George Lucas to do so. Making fan edits and indie movies based on the Original Trilogy do not count as creativity. They are just stealing a brilliant idea from a more successful person and modifying it to fit someone else's tastes. The original movies were not perfect -- they have always had flaws -- but if the man who created them feels that the current versions are the best versions, that is his prerogative as their creator. If you don't like them, create your own multi-billion dollar franchise, and make them your way. These are his movies, and as long as they are his, they are mine too. Simple arithmetic. The combined total runtime of the entire saga is 805 minutes, 418 for the Prequels, 387 for the Original Trilogy. The only noticeable changes to the prequels were the aforementioned CG Yoda, and a brief fly-through of Coruscant, both in Episode â  (the fly-through was included on previous home video versions). B003ZSJ212|5.0|\\"This will be a day long remembered.\\" This quote from the first Star Wars film, known since 1981 as \\"Episode â£: A New Hope,\\" describes the feelings of myself, and countless other Star Wars fans on the eagerly awaited Blu-ray release of the Star Wars saga.  I just received mine about two hours ago as of this writing.  But with every new release of Star Wars comes a looming question: What will George Lucas change this time?<br /><br />This was not always the case.  Fifteen years of home video releases saw the trilogy presented exactly as they were seen in theaters (albeit usually in panned and scanned versions formatted to fit a standard television screen), and with the release of 1993's THX edition release (available in widescreen for the first time on VHS), it seemed the movies would never be better than they were.<br /><br />The work Industrial Light & Magic did for Steven Spielberg's Jurassic Park changed all that.  Lucas admitted that he was never happy with the way Star Wars came out, due mostly to restraints of time, money, and ILM's pioneering, yet crude technology, and announced that with current computer graphics, he could finally go back and complete scenes from the original trilogy that had bugged him for years.<br /><br />1997 saw the release of the Star Wars Trilogy Special Edition, a theatrical (and eventually home video) re-release of all three films with newly enhanced special effects, and a handful of additional scenes.  To say the reaction was mixed would be an understatement.  Nearly a decade-and-a-half after they were revealed to the world, many of the changes Lucas made have been heavily criticized and hotly debated.  Those who grew up on the originals felt that the new additions ruined the movies.  Disappointed fans took solace in the fact that at least they could still watch the original versions of the films at home on VHS.<br /><br />Seven years later saw the DVD release of the \\"Original Trilogy\\" as it was now known, following two prequels,with one last film on the way.  Fans pleaded and prayed that Lucas would see fit to release the theatrical versions alongside the Special Editions, which were now said to have even more changes to better tie in the Originals with the Prequels.  Not only did they not get their wish (at least not until a few years later), but many fans scoffed at some of the newest changes made to the series, believing the prequels to be further ruining the series, and any changes made to accommodate those films would be even more detrimental to the dignity of the Original Trilogy.<br /><br />So here we are today.  I have my Star Wars Blu-rays laying on my sofa, awaiting their weekend marathon.  I have heard rumors of even more changes to Episodes â£ through â¥, as well as a few for â  through â¢.  These rumors include deleted scenes incorporated into the films, a new CG Yoda to replace the puppet (I knew they were doing this for Episode â , though I'm still unsure about whether this plan also includes Episodes â¤ and â¥), and the possibility of Han once again shooting first.  I will thank anyone who has knowledge of exactly what changes have been made to keep quiet.  I want to be surprised.<br /><br />The thing is, after all these years of changes and complaints, I still don't see what the big deal is.  There are very few changes to the Original Trilogy to which I strongly object, and none of those changes affect the over-all film in any way.  What difference does it make whether Han or Greedo shoots first?  As long as Greedo misses, the other approximately 13 hours, 20 minutes, and 59Â¾ seconds of the saga remains roughly the same.  Sure, the Jabba the Hutt scene in A New Hope seems redundant and unnecessary in terms of the single film, and maybe it ruins the \\"big reveal\\" of Jabba in Return of the Jedi, but since Jabba already appears in The Phantom Menace (canonically the first film in the saga), and that scene was always supposed to be in A New Hope in the first place, ithardly ruins anything. B00000DWE2|5.0|Given the Who's reputation for being among the bad boys of the 60's, smashing instruments and lights and blowing up drums, who could have guessed that they would create one of the most cohesive pieces of rock music ever, a concept album, an opera, and plain solid rock.  The story is relatively easy to follow, with a variety of sordid elements, the details of which can be found in a host of places, and which have been covered by other reviewers, so let's focus on the music.<br /><br />The music holds up well; more than well, it was well done, and remains one of my favorites.  Released originally in 1969, the album had the benefit of building upon ground broken by the Beatles, among a host of others.  The Beatles infused elements of classical music into rock.  The Moody Blues fused classical music and rock into one.  The Who raised the ante by creating a new \\"classical\\" opera where the music was rock instead of classical.<br /><br />An example of some of the best instrumental neo-classical rock on this album is track 5, \\"Sparks,\\" which has excellent bass, is definably rock, and yet retains the complexity and flavor of a classical work, in rock music form.<br /><br />Among the other songs that are definably rock are \\"I'm Free,\\" \\"Tommy Can You Hear Me,\\" \\"Eyesight to the Blind,\\" \\"We're Not Gonna Take It,\\" and \\"Pinball Wizard.\\"<br /><br />Other songs that fill in the story that have the flavor of a musical are songs like \\"It's a Boy\\" and \\"1921.\\"  One song that has elements of a Beatles song is \\"Christmas.\\"<br /><br />I will allow the reader to categorize the other songs.  Truly it's not important.  What is important is that the Who took a variety of musical elements, classical, rock, opera, and created a master work of music.  Music that tells the poignant story of a child abused by his mother and step-father after witnessing the murder of the boy's father.  Thrown in are classic elements of opera: infidelity, exploitation, and cowardice, along with elements explored at that time in very few works: incest, homosexuality, and sexual abuse.<br /><br />As you listen to each of the tracks, discover that the Who were able to infuse multiple styles of rock music into the various songs, because each song allowed a variety of styles to enhance the subject of each song.  Hard rock, instrumental, pop, harmonies, raucous solos, hard-driving riffs, heavy bass, horns, it is all here, and more.<br /><br />Has \\"Tommy\\" dated well since 1969?  I think so.  The essence of the sound remains the Who, and if you know Who music at all, much of it sounds current, three decades and more after it was written.  \\"Tommy\\" is no less current.  Listening to the \\"The Acid Queen,\\" I hear music that some of today's artists are trying to achieve: a more basic rock sound, steering away from complex synthesizers and an over-orchestrated, over-exaggerated rock sound.<br /><br />Okay, where is the drawback?  The album really needs to be listened to in its entirety.  Some of the songs and lyrics make little sense outside the context of the album.  But given that the album is an opera, it makes sense that the album needs listened to as an album.  I only listen to \\"Tommy\\" as an album, except for the stray tracks on collections.<br /><br />\\"Tommy\\" is another of the numerous works of rock that were very experimental at the time it was released.  \\"Tommy\\" succeeded in being very good rock, and broke more new ground for rock music.  It has aged well, and continues to be one of my favorites.  It is the Who.  How could you go wrong with the Who?sexuality, and sexual abuse. B000002J6H|5.0|Stephen Stills, as most everyone reading these reviews knows, is one-third of CS&N, and one-fourth of CSN&Y.  He is also clearly the Mozart of the group, writing many of the most memorable songs and playing and recording most of the instrumental parts, and with such mastery, that this cohorts nicknamed him \\"Captain Manyhands.\\"  In short, in terms of pure musicianship he is, contrary to the Amazon reviewer's statement, easily THE most accomplished member of the bunch.  Anyway, on to the review:     I called this the \\"Solo\\" album in the \\"Classic\\" sense of the word because it seems to be the work of a BAND musician (at heart) stepping away from his main group to make something much more personal.   He's.. say, given his \\"regular\\" band most of his top-tier stuff, but he's got some songs in the vault that are still close to his heart, and he wants to record them.  And this wonderful album is the result.  If the songs don't quite reach the same heights as \\"Carry On\\", Suite Judy Blue Eyes\\", they are, nevertheless, beautiful to the ears, and full of great musical and lyrical touches.  Stills can jam (even with himself) and it makes a gorgeous noise, the guitars, bass, and organ tearing it up on every cut.       The first track, \\"Love the One You're With\\" is a giddy ode to adultery.  It's bark is perhaps bigger than its bite, and with all its heavy instrumentation and over-the-top choral cheerleading, remains a rather lightweight little anthem.  Great for singalongs though, and has a very infectious chorus that has kept it around even after all these years.      \\"Do for the Others\\" is much more interesting tune, and the melody winds its way around the chord changes in all sorts of amazing ways.  This is the kind of cut that sounds very.. Stills.       \\"Church\\" gives him a chance to experiment some more with his \\"cast of 1000's\\" approach.  This is one the Ray Charles could have done a great cover of.  After many hearings, I find this recording of it to be, for me, a bit too heavy on the \\"epic\\" side.   If the song was a little more interesting, compositionally, perhaps the mega-production might have been a better fit.   I'd love to hear a remix one day with 1/3rd as many people on it.       The two following cuts again illustrate the brilliance of Stills when he's got similarly gifted collaborators to work off and push against.  Again, the songs taken by themselves, are very, very good, if not necessarily hallowed classics.  Still the way they're brought off by Stills and his guests Eric Clapton and Jimi Hendrix elevate them to what can only be described as absolutely jaw-dropping killer tracks.  Stills knew exactly how to work these guys into his musical vision, while giving them a fresh backdrop for their own great talents.   Everyone here just shines, and makes these among the strongest cuts on the album.      \\"Sit Yourself Down\\" is another rousing sing-along, but perhaps less interesting than \\"Love the One..\\" or \\"Church\\".  Not bad for a side 2 opener (for those who still play their old vinyl copies).  Still very fun, and a good one to crank up when you're alone in the house, doing chores, and want to feel like you've got company just in the next room.      \\"To a Flame\\"~This one is another bit of pure introspective Stills, and with its great moody atmospherics and beautifully strange chord movement.  Never tire of hearing it.      \\"Black Queen\\"~Stills has recorded this one more than once, but I believe he saved his best take for this album.  The song, simply a rather loose collection of word-plays and vocal/guitar Blues runs, is wielded into the masterpiece it is strictly by its fiery performance, and great sense of drama and atmosphere.  Stills, the solo Blues-man is at his absolute best here.   Ironic that the least-realized actual song on this album, in terms of its ability to stand alone and apart from its performance, is also its strongest cut.  This is definitely the one track I'd play for anyone who hasn't heard it, and wants to know if Stills was ever really \\"All That\\" back in the day...    \\"Cherokee\\" is a song that introduces some Jazzier touches, including flute and sax solo breaks played by Sidney George.  The rhythm section includes Booker T. Jones on keys.  \\"We Are Not Helpless\\" is Stills' response to Neil Young's song \\"Helpless.\\"  These two songs are, well, very much of their time; you can almost smell the incense and feel the vibe of bell-bottomed guitar player/singers around you.     Overall, a very good collection of tunes performed by the musical shaman of CSN (and sometimes Y), with a little help from his awesome friends, and lots of recording tape.         Michael

As far as I know, it was previously only available on a long out-of-print VHS tape.<br /><br />If you're a fan of the movie, this is certainly a must-have (especially at the current low price).  But even folks who are just fans of horror cinema in general should pick it up for Terror in the Aisles.  Aside from the aforementioned technical deficits, it's a welcome addition to my Blu-ray collection.

B002X7FELS|3.0|I had been told great things about this novel, so even though the premise (a guy pretending to choke to get his saviours to give him money out of pity) seemed funky (in the bad sense), I gave it a shot. And by \\"expecting\\", I don't mean I knew what the book was going to be, but in a general sense, I was not surprised.     - narrative in present tense, first person, check  - lots of trivia about specialised knowledge, in this case medical knowledge, diseases, etc, check  - important character that turns out to be something else, check  - starting declarative sentences with  \\"what, where, etc\\", as in \\"What you have to do when you're here, etc.\\" \\"Where you're supposed to be is, etc,\\" (as in Invisible Monsters)    There are others, but I found these in every single novel by Palahniuk I've read. I'm not saying it's a bad thing, but this may explain why I didn't like Choke as much as I liked the others.    There's always a touch of above the top in Palahniuk's novels, which I usually don't mind and take it as a satiric rather than a \\"realistic\\" approach, although that's always done in measured ways, but with this novel, the typical character-twist, I couldn't buy. There's over the top and there's plain impossible. I won't go into details to avoid spoilers, but that one was far-fetched.    While not a bad novel, I liked it far, far less than Survivor. Denny is an endearing character; Colonial Dunsboro is pretty cool a location and context, and there are some very relevant quotes with regards to our time. Let me see if I can find one... Or maybe you will enjoy it better than I did even if you have read other novels by the same author. Who knows.    Finally, I think my main negative impression was that the novel isn't focused enough, perhaps. There's a bit of all the following things in there, without any of them being thoroughly used, I felt:    - sex addictions (I expected more weird obsessions and urban legends and such)  - crazy mothers / sons and mothers  - men and women in modern times, and their relationship  - Christ (made me wish that anatomical skinned human had been raising his arms to look like Christ, would have been a terrific cover)    And others I forget. Would I have preferred to have only one of these themes used at the exclusion of the others? No. I'm not suggesting I could have written a better version of that novel, I'm only trying to precise my reaction to it. I have a hard time knowing exactly why I didn't like as much as others by the same author. My best guess remains that it's so similar to his other novels that I've been somewhat blunted by now. Maybe I shouldn't read Palanhiuk novels in rows.    Still a commendable book for the average Palahniuk reader.

For Anyone - For Anyone is the upbeat acoustic ditty we've come to expect from Liam Gallagher since the success of Songbird. His simple lyrics while a sticking point on Little James became endearing and nearly inimitable on tracks like Meaning Of Soul or I Believe In All. Here he falls a bit short from the standard he set himself but it won't keep it from being covered by guitar-wielding beach-dwellers hoping to sleep with doe-eyed bikini-clad idyllists. Cheers to them.<br /><br />8.  Kill For A Dream - Here we seem to clearly have a Hurricane #1 track.  For those of you too young to remember a band with almost no hits, they were another act signed to the now defunct but legendary Creation Records. If I recall correctly, there was the inkling of a rivalry between singer Alex Lowe from the Andy Bell-led band and Liam Gallagher. Now we can definitively say, Liam would've sung those songs just as well if not better. Sorry, Mister Lowe. And yes, this song was penned by Andy Bell.<br /><br />9.  Standing On The Edge Of Noise - SOTEON sounds like the audition tape for a band hoping to ghost-write for Noel Gallagher. The vocal effect is similar to the faraway vocals Noel uses on tracks with the Chemical Brothers (Setting Sun or Let Forever Be). The song feels a bit like the Small Faces` Here Comes The Nice crossed with something Slade.<br /><br />10.  Wigwam - Wigwam is almost a Polyphonic Spree-type trip into psychedelia. Liam's Death In Vegas colloboration, Scorpio Rising, has a remix performed by the robe-wearing hippie choir and maybe Liam liked the feel they gave it. The song sounds slightly out of place though refreshing for an unexpected turn. Maybe Liam plans to front the Spree or lesser known contemporaries The Secret Powers And The Electric Family Choir. The Leader urges me to remind you, cult rock is forever.<br /><br />11.  Three Ring Circus - A Gem-penned rocker without the bite of Hung In A Bad Place or the heart of A Bell Will Ring.  Not a bad tune, just not a good one.<br /><br />12.  The Beat Goes On - Hands down, the best track on the album. Andy Bell has his best songwriting in a tune where Liam sounds brilliant and not a single note is misplaced. If Beady Eye never make another record, The Beat Goes On will still be the tune played as the encore to the \\"gig in the sky\\" and should be considered a requiem to the great, great band that was oasis.<br /><br />13.  The Morning Son - Echoe-y vocals create the ambience for a Liam-penned answer to Acquiesce. Noel steadfastly said Acquiesce was a song about friendship and not brotherhood and Liam will probably say something similar about this song. \\"You're blinded by what you idolise... He's in my mind, he's in my soul, he's even in my rock n' roll... You go your way and I'll go mine.\\" Here's hoping the brothers find a way to bury the past.hout the bite of Hung In A Bad Place or the heart of A Bell Will Ring.  Not a bad tune, just not a good one.     12.  The Beat Goes On - Hands down, the best track on the album. B005974CNA|5.0|Any fan of Dream Theater no doubt has strong opinions on the direction that their sound has been going in recently, the departure of founding member Mike Portnoy, and the continuation of the band with new drummer Mike Mangini. I'm no exception. Personally, I thought Dream Theater's last two albums were crap. Dream Theater has always been a heavy band, however they are first and foremost a progressive band. There is a variety of sound in their music that sets them apart from other bands. On Chaos and Black Clouds they seemed to have lost those unique elements and simply tried to make albums for metal fans. I do not think this works for Dream Theater, and this disappointed me as a fan. When Portnoy left, I tended to side with him, thinking that yes, maybe the band would benefit from a break from each other. When the band posted their videos chronicling their search for a drummer, I didn't pay attention. When they released the first song, \\"On the Backs of Angels,\\" I had pretty much already written off the album, listened to the song once, and dismissed it as the same crap that had been released on the previous two albums. Unfortunately, my fandome would not let me completely dismiss the band, and I was forced by some unknown reason (perhaps curiosity) to purchase the new album a few days before its official release from an awesome independent record store that shall not be named. (Support your local record stores, kids!) My expectations for this album could not have possibly been lower. I was pleasantly surprised.<br /><br />Everything that I used to love about Dream Theater has made its way back onto this album. Who knows, maybe Portnoy was the problem in the band, because the songwriting seems fresher, more natural, and just... better. There is a lot of experimentation with song structures, keys, and timing on this album. The melodies are strong. Overall, the album is still on the heavy side, but not too heavy. The solo sections sound like they compliment the songs overall instead of feeling out of place and contrived like they did on Chaos and Black Clouds. Those two albums seemed to basically be about who could masturbate with their instruments the best. With A Dramatic Turn of Events, Dream Theater has turned their focus back to the music.<br /><br />Listening to the album now, I don't see how I could have not liked \\"On the Backs of Angels\\" when I first heard it... perhaps I was still a bit jaded... or maybe it just needed more than the one listen that I gave it. It is an excellent song in classic DT fashion. \\"Build Me Up, Break Me Down\\" has a sound that is reminiscent of LaBrie's solo albums. It's heavy, and has a strong chorus. \\"Bridges in the Sky\\" is probably my favorite song on the album. It's a complex song, its heavy, it's melodic, it has a strong hook; it's everything that I love about Dream Theater. The heavy songs are complimented by the ballads. While on Chaos and Black Clouds, the ballads seemed like they were almost written for the radio, the ballads on A Dramatic Turn of Events seem to compliment the rest of the album. I definitely hear elements of Images and Words, Scenes from a Memory, and Six Degrees on this album, however while there are passages that seem to be influenced by previous work, Dream Theater does a good job to not rip off their previous work.<br /><br />A Dramatic Turn of Events is an album that you need to listen to a few times to begin to really appreciate it. Even now, having only had the album for about a week, I feel as though I might not have had enough time with it to truly give it a proper review. I feel that 4 stars is a fairly accurate rating for it as I really am liking it a lot, but I do not feel like it is in the same category as albums like Scenes, Images, Six Degrees, or Octavarium.... though, who knows... give me another month of listening to it and maybe I'll have it up there. The album definitely seems to get better with every listen. One thing is for sure: As a long time DreamTheater fan, I am incredibly happy with this release. As cliche as the phrase is... A Dramatic Turn of Events represents a return to form for Dream Theater.<br /><br />---<br />UPDATE<br />---<br /><br />I'm coming back to this review and updating it after several months of listening to this album. As I stated in the last paragraph of my review above, this album indeed gets better with every listen. I absolutely love this album now and am regarding it as one of their best albums. I am changing my rating of 4-stars to 5-stars. If you haven't already, pick this album up. And go see them on the summer tour!Theater fan, I am incredibly happy with this release. As cliche as the phrase is... A Dramatic Turn of Events represents a return to form for Dream Theater.<br /><br />---<br />UPDATE<br />---<br /><br />I'm coming back to this review and updating it after several months of listening to this album. As I stated in the last paragraph of my review above, this album indeed gets better with every listen. I absolutely love this album now and am regarding it as one of their best albums. B004LROMSO|3.0|King Arthur seems to be rather a difficult subject to adapt. For me, not one of them has really done it well, but a few come close. Excalibur is a stand out, Mist of Avalon isn't too bad, and even Sam Neill's Merlin tries hard. The recent King Arthur starring Clive Owen was fun, but really a retread of The Seven Samurai.    One of the major problems, is what do you put in and what do you leave out? Do you set it in a medieval fantasy world or in the grim Roman-Britain dark ages? How much magic do you put in? Is it real or pagan religion?    This adaptation is rather poorly thought out. The stand out problem for me right off the bat are the costumes. The ladies do all right in nebulous renaissance fair outfits, but the men fare poorly. There is hardly any armour and virtually no helmets. This makes the whole thing look cheap and rather silly. Even dark ages Britain went in for armour, if not as much as the later ages. The story of King Arthur, for me, is about KNIGHTS IN ARMOUR, and that's what I expect. Instead you have rather floppy hairstyled young men running around in baggy shirts claiming to be the champions of the king. I don't believe them, they look rather wet. There is no sense that this is King Arthur, these are his KNIGHTS and he is a military force to be reckoned with in the land.    The magic aspect is poor. Morgan can do an impressive spell and make herself look like some one else. Impressive. Merlin can burn people and turn lakes to ice in minutes. Very impressive. So magic, powerful magic, exists in this world. So why go for nonmagical explanations about the Sword in the Stone or Excalibur? The treatment of the Sword in the Stone would work in an adaptation with no 'real' magic, but not here. The Excalibur adaptation is worse. Merlin gets the sword for Arthur in a very mundane way (from a weaponsmith), kills the smith, chases the smith's daughter (named Excalibur?!) to her death out on the lake and prises the sword from her dead hand as she sinks beneath the ice that Merlin magically turned the lake into. How bloody complicated! How dumb! The story's already there, why not just do that instead?     The cast are OK and there are a quite a few shots of attractive naked ladies on view for those of us who like that sort of thing. Joseph Fiennes and Claire Forlani seem to be trying to act being each other in their scenes together. Fortunately Forlani has long hair, so you can tell them apart. Actually, that's kind of mean for me to say that, but their scenes are rather tedious. The whole Arthur, Guinevere, Guinevere's so-what husband character plot line is lame too. It just wastes storytelling time.    The best thing in this is Eva Green. She does a great job. Pity the show around her is lame.    So, all in all, a wasted opportunity. So much gold in Malory, so much drek here. I did watch this right after watching Game of Thrones, but even without that excellent series fresh in my mind, this is LAME.

B004F9OV5C|4.0|Adam Kindred, a British climatologist who has been working at an American university, returns to London for a job interview. A chance encounter in a Chelsea restaurant gets him involved in a brutal murder, and some foolish steps taken in panic quickly result in his becoming a wanted man. What Adam does not at first realize is that this is not merely a police case; he has unknowingly stumbled upon information that could derail a multi-billion dollar pharmaceutical launch, and the company has deadly resources at its disposal. The opening chapters show how astoundingly quickly a hitherto upstanding citizen can be reduced to nothing, barely able to keep from going under at the waterline of society.<br /><br />Literally so. Almost all the story takes place by the River Thames: in a triangle of waste land by Chelsea Bridge where Adam hangs out, in the depot of the river police where a young policewoman starts making inquiries of her own, in the wards and morgues of the riverside hospitals, in a brutal block of high-rise apartments where Adam develops an unexpected relationship with a prostitute, in the gleaming new office buildings built in the former docklands. Boyd's intimate knowledge of the city and its river does much to counteract the unrelieved bleakness of the long middle portion of this book. He also imagines a wide range of characters from the homeless at a bizarre religious soup kitchen, through an assortment of slum bosses and professional killers, to sleek boardroom manipulators. And Adam, though stripped of resources, is by no means stupid.<br /><br />Towards the end of the book, Adam realizes \\"that it was impossible for everything to be all right in this complicated, difficult, mortal life we lead.\\" It is a realization that accords with Boyd's obvious interest in the fragility of identity (see also his [[ASIN:0375702164 ARMADILLO]]) and his moral relativism. Those looking for the nice clean ending of a traditional thriller will not find it here, and our hero is by no means heroic throughout. But it is a satisfying outcome and -- after the occasional suspension of disbelief required earlier -- a surprisingly realistic one.heroic throughout. 0385523394|3.0|Piper Kernan gives the reader a good view of what life is like in a women's minimum security prison.  She goes into great detail on the day-to-day existence of an inmate.  The jobs, food, visitation, guards, and recreational activities are all very well described.  However, this book must be read with a critical eye to fully appreciate it.  The reader must understand that Kernan is trying to put herself in the most positive light possible, and what she does not say is very important, too.    For example, Kernan says that she only delivered drug money once, and that was the extent of her involvement with her lesbian lover's drug operation.  However, on page 22, in describing the evidence against her, Kernan says \\"the evidence the government had gathered against me - it included a detailed statement from her that described me carrying cash to Europe.\\"  She wants the reader to believe that she pled guilty on the basis of only an admitted drug dealer's statement and nothing more. However, The key word is \\"included.\\" That means the government had a lot more evidence that Kernan does not share with the reader. It is doubtful that her very expensive lawyer would have allowed her to take the plea if that was all they had.  It is far more likely that Kernan was much more deeply involved in her lover's drug operation that she reveals and that the government really had the goods on her.        Also, in the \\"Questions for Discussion\\" on page 325, question 3 begins with \\"Piper is indicted for a crime she committed several years earlier and is sentenced to more than a year in prison.\\" There is absolutely nothing in that sentence that tells the reader that Kernan pled guilty of her own accord.  It tries to give the impression that if a person is indicted, the government can send them to prison without a trial.  This is juvenile in its effort to make Kernan appear to be the victim of an evil government that is out of control.      Kernan never seems to say that what she did was wrong.  It is not until page 180 that Kernan finally admits that what she did helped to increase the suffering of the female drug addicts that she was incarcerated with.  But, Kernan only gives her guilt trip one short paragragh before going back to her theme that society is really the villain here and that these unfortunate women should be given counseling and treatment rather than prison terms.  She completely overlooks the fact that most of these women committed other crimes to support their drug habit.    This brings us to the biggest error in Kernan's reasoning.  Throughout the book, she insists that drug offenses like hers are non-violent crimes.  She completely ignores the fact that many burglaries, muggings, and car-jackings are committed by addicts trying to get the money to buy drugs from people like Kernan and her lover.  The money she carried to Europe probably included the life savings of some elderly woman, stolen to get drug money. But, Kernan ignores this because she did not steal the old lady's money herself.  Her refusal to face this fact is probably due to her super-liberal, priviliged upbringing, which allowed her to close her eyes to the unpleasant realities of life. She just doesn't think about it, and that makes it OK in her mind.    Finally, Kernan's life was not negatively impacted at all by her incerceration.  After being released, she tells us \\"Work began a week later, at a marketing job created just for me at a tech company that was run by a friend.\\"  Must be nice to have a friend who can make a job just for her.  Of course, he did make wait a whole week for it.  Ah, the problems and miseries of the idle rich.

I agree that the \\"My recommendation\\" sections for each setting are most useful and clearly explain why you would probably want to select the same settings as the author, but he also explains why some one with a different shooting style or needs would select another choice instead. I think that so far I either already did choose the same setting or chose it after reading this section. But other things don't really fit my style or workflow (like the file naming convention Darrell advocates for example).    It is a hefty volume though for sure, so not something I will want to carry with me on a trip for example. But after reading this book I think the manual will certainly jog my memory if need be.
